\begin{lemma}[Boundary-near exclusion for both directional kernels at a generic turning boundary]
\label{lem:boundary-near-directional-kernel-exclusion}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Define
\[
K_{21}(s,t):=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t)),
\qquad
K_{12}(t,s):=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s)),
\]
with
\[
F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h)).
\]
Then there is a neighborhood of the boundary pair on which both directional
kernels are nonzero:
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0,
\qquad
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\]
for \(s\in I_2\), \(t\in I_1\) sufficiently close to \(h_b\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Write
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
y(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}.
\]
Since
\[
y'(h)=\frac{N(h)}{c(h)^2},
\]
the hypothesis \(N(h_b)\neq 0\) implies that \(y\) is a valid local
coordinate near \(h_b\). Thus the normalized planar curve may be written
locally as a graph
\[
x=\chi(y).
\]
At \(h_b\),
\[
\frac{dx}{dy}=\frac{M(h)}{N(h)},
\]
so
\[
\left.\frac{dx}{dy}\right|_{h_b}=0.
\]
Differentiating once more,
\[
\frac{d^2x}{dy^2}
=
\frac{c(h)^2}{N(h)}\cdot\frac{M'(h)N(h)-M(h)N'(h)}{N(h)^2},
\]
and since \(M(h_b)=0\),
\[
\left.\frac{d^2x}{dy^2}\right|_{h_b}
=
\frac{c(h_b)^2\,M'(h_b)}{N(h_b)^2}\neq 0.
\]
Hence, after shrinking to a small neighborhood of \(h_b\), the graph
\(x=\chi(y)\) is strictly convex or strictly concave.

For such a graph, the tangent line at any point meets the graph only at its
point of tangency. Therefore no tangent line from one side can meet the
opposite side inside a sufficiently small neighborhood of the boundary.
Equivalently, both directional tangent-separator kernels are nonzero near the
boundary pair.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Mirrored first adjacent zero implies tangency]
\label{lem:mirrored-first-adjacent-zero-implies-tangency}
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\), \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be compact interior subintervals
across a generic turning boundary, and define
\[
K_{12}(t,s):=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s)),
\qquad
t\in I_1,\ s\in I_2.
\]
Assume every zero of \(K_{12}\) on \(I_1\times I_2\) lies in
\(J_1\times J_2\). Let \((t_*,s_*)\in J_1\times J_2\) be a zero whose
\(t\)-coordinate is minimal in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all zeros of
\(K_{12}\) on \(I_1\times I_2\). Then
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Because \(M\neq 0\) on the open interval \(I_1\), the function
\[
x(h)=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)}
\]
is a valid local coordinate on \(I_1\). Since every zero of \(K_{12}\) lies in
the compact interior rectangle \(J_1\times J_2\), the chosen point
\((t_*,s_*)\) is interior in the ambient domain \(I_1\times I_2\).

Suppose
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)\neq 0.
\]
Then by the implicit function theorem there is a \(C^1\) local zero curve
\[
s=s(t)
\]
through \((t_*,s_*)\), defined for \(t\) near \(t_*\), such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s(t))=0.
\]
Because \(t_*\) is interior in \(I_1\), there are nearby points with smaller
\(x=v_5/c\) ordering still in \(I_1\). For such \(t\), the point
\((t,s(t))\) is also a zero of \(K_{12}\), contradicting the minimality of
\(t_*\). Therefore
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Conditional symmetric directional-kernel exclusion across turning boundaries]
\label{thm:conditional-symmetric-directional-kernel-exclusion}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the zero-capture hypothesis required in
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity} for the kernel
\(K_{21}\) on \(I_2\times I_1\);
\item the symmetric zero-capture hypothesis for \(K_{12}\), namely that every
zero of
\[
K_{12}(t,s)=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s))
\]
on \(I_1\times I_2\) lies in some compact interior rectangle
\(J_1\times J_2\Subset I_1\times I_2\).
\end{enumerate}
Then both directional kernels are zero-free:
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad (s\in I_2,\ t\in I_1),
\]
and
\[
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\qquad (t\in I_1,\ s\in I_2).
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The first assertion is exactly
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}.

For the second assertion, boundary-near exclusion for \(K_{12}\) is provided by
Lemma~\ref{lem:boundary-near-directional-kernel-exclusion}. Assume for
contradiction that \(K_{12}\) has a zero. By the symmetric zero-capture
hypothesis, every zero lies in a compact interior rectangle \(J_1\times J_2\).
Choose a zero \((t_*,s_*)\in J_1\times J_2\) whose \(t\)-coordinate is minimal
in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all zeros of \(K_{12}\) on \(I_1\times I_2\).

By Lemma~\ref{lem:mirrored-first-adjacent-zero-implies-tangency},
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
Together with
\[
K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0,
\]
this implies that the tangent line at \(\Gamma(t_*)\) is also tangent at
\(\Gamma(s_*)\). Hence the two adjacent intervals share a common interior
tangent slope, contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}. Therefore
\(K_{12}\) has no zero.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Status of the directional-kernel reduction]
\label{rem:status-directional-kernel-reduction}
Lemma~\ref{lem:pair-pair-gives-directional-kernel-zero} reduces an adjacent
pair-pair line across a generic turning boundary to a compact interior zero of
one of the two directional tangent-separator kernels. Theorem~\ref{thm:conditional-symmetric-directional-kernel-exclusion}
shows that the remaining turning-boundary issue is no longer orientation
selection between \(K_{21}\) and \(K_{12}\), but the symmetric zero-capture
problem for these two kernels.
\end{remark}

The right replacement target is:

    pair-pair-generated zero-capture, not full zero-capture.

Yes ‚ÄĒ I think there is a real way to bypass full symmetric zero-capture.

The key is to replace ‚Äúcapture all zeros of the kernel on the whole open rectangle‚ÄĚ with a much weaker and more relevant statement:

    for a fixed compact opposite branch J1‚čźI1J1‚Äč‚čźI1‚Äč, tangencies from the adjacent branch I2I2‚Äč that are sufficiently close to the shared turning boundary cannot hit J1J1‚Äč at all.

That gives a strip exclusion lemma, and once you have that, the compact first-contact argument works on the actual compact overlap family coming from pair-pair, with no need to capture arbitrary stray zeros elsewhere.

So the attack I think works is this.
1. Prove a strip exclusion lemma

x‚Ä≤(hb‚Äč)=0,x‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(hb‚Äč)ÓÄ†=0,y‚Ä≤(hb‚Äč)ÓÄ†=0,

so after using yy as local parameter, the normalized curve is locally
x=Ōá(y)
x=Ōá(y)

with Ōá‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(0)‚Č†0Ōá‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(0)ÓÄ†=0. Thus xx has a strict local extremum at the boundary, and the tangent slope

m(y)=dxdy‚Äč=Ōá‚Ä≤(y)1‚Äč

blows up one-sidedly as y‚Üí0¬Īy‚Üí0¬Ī.

‚ą£x(t)‚ąíx(hb)‚ą£‚Č•2őī.
‚ą£x(t)‚ąíx(hb‚Äč)‚ą£‚Č•2őī.

For s‚ąąI2s‚ąąI2‚Äč sufficiently near hbhb‚Äč,
‚ą£x(s)‚ąíx(hb)‚ą£‚Č§őī,
‚ą£x(s)‚ąíx(hb‚Äč)‚ą£‚Č§őī,

so
‚ą£x(t)‚ąíx(s)‚ą£‚Č•őī(t‚ąąJ1).
‚ą£x(t)‚ąíx(s)‚ą£‚Č•őī(t‚ąąJ1‚Äč).

y(t)‚ąíy(s)=m(s)(x(t)‚ąíx(s)).
y(t)‚ąíy(s)=m(s)(x(t)‚ąíx(s)).

y(t)‚ąíy(s)

x(t)‚ąíx(s)
x(t)‚ąíx(s)

stays bounded away from 00. Therefore the right-hand side has arbitrarily large magnitude and fixed sign, while the left-hand side stays bounded. So equality is impossible for ss sufficiently close to hbhb‚Äč.

Hence
K21(s,t)‚Č†0
K21‚Äč(s,t)ÓÄ†=0

for all s‚ąąU2, t‚ąąJ1s‚ąąU2‚Äč, t‚ąąJ1‚Äč.

Lemma (strip exclusion for K12K12‚Äč)

For every compact J2‚čźI2J2‚Äč‚čźI2‚Äč, there exists a one-sided neighborhood U1‚äāI1U1‚Äč‚äāI1‚Äč of the boundary such that
K12(t,s)=det‚Ā°(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F(s))‚Č†0(t‚ąąU1, s‚ąąJ2).

This is the crucial replacement for full zero-capture.
2. Use strip exclusion to get a compact first-contact theorem

Now suppose the bridge lemma has already produced a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels, say K21K21‚Äč, on a compact pair
J2‚čźI2,J1‚čźI1.
J2‚Äč‚čźI2‚Äč,J1‚Äč‚čźI1‚Äč.

Enlarge J2J2‚Äč slightly toward the shared boundary so that its boundary-facing collar lies in the strip U2U2‚Äč from the previous lemma, while still remaining compact and interior.

Define the compact zero set
Z21:={(s,t)‚ąąJ2√óJ1: K21(s,t)=0}.
Z21‚Äč:={(s,t)‚ąąJ2‚Äč√óJ1‚Äč: K21‚Äč(s,t)=0}.

If Z21‚Č†‚ąÖZ21‚ÄčÓÄ†=‚ąÖ, let
A21:={s‚ąąJ2:‚ąÉ‚ÄČt‚ąąJ1 with K21(s,t)=0}.
A21‚Äč:={s‚ąąJ2‚Äč:‚ąÉt‚ąąJ1‚Äč with K21‚Äč(s,t)=0}.

Since Z21Z21‚Äč is compact, A21A21‚Äč is compact. Since the boundary-facing collar of J2J2‚Äč is zero-free against all t‚ąąJ1t‚ąąJ1‚Äč, the set A21A21‚Äč is bounded away from that collar. So A21A21‚Äč has a boundary-nearest element s‚ąós‚ąó‚Äč that is still an interior point of J2J2‚Äč. Pick t‚ąó‚ąąJ1t‚ąó‚Äč‚ąąJ1‚Äč with
K21(s‚ąó,t‚ąó)=0.
K21‚Äč(s‚ąó‚Äč,t‚ąó‚Äč)=0.

Now argue exactly as in the old first-zero lemma, but only inside this compact family:

if
‚ąātK21(s‚ąó,t‚ąó)‚Č†0,
‚ąāt‚ÄčK21‚Äč(s‚ąó‚Äč,t‚ąó‚Äč)ÓÄ†=0,

then the implicit function theorem gives a local zero curve
t=t(s)
t=t(s)

through (s‚ąó,t‚ąó)(s‚ąó‚Äč,t‚ąó‚Äč), so for ss slightly boundary-nearer than s‚ąós‚ąó‚Äč, still inside J2J2‚Äč, we get another zero (s,t(s))‚ąąZ21(s,t(s))‚ąąZ21‚Äč, contradicting the minimality of s‚ąós‚ąó‚Äč in A21A21‚Äč.

Therefore
‚ąātK21(s‚ąó,t‚ąó)=0.
‚ąāt‚ÄčK21‚Äč(s‚ąó‚Äč,t‚ąó‚Äč)=0.

Together with
K21(s‚ąó,t‚ąó)=0,
K21‚Äč(s‚ąó‚Äč,t‚ąó‚Äč)=0,

this means the tangent line at őď(s‚ąó)őď(s‚ąó‚Äč) is also tangent at őď(t‚ąó)őď(t‚ąó‚Äč).

So from a compact directional-kernel zero we get a common tangent line across the adjacent branches, without any global zero-capture theorem.

The same argument mirrors for K12K12‚Äč.
3. This gives the actual bypass theorem

So the theorem to prove next is:
Theorem (compact first-contact exclusion for pair-pair-generated directional zeros)

Let I1<I2I1‚Äč<I2‚Äč be adjacent across a generic turning boundary. Suppose a finite-slope line is 22-secant to both branches. Then the support-overlap truncation and overlap-endpoint lemma produce a compact interior zero of one of K21K21‚Äč or K12K12‚Äč. For either direction, the strip exclusion lemma and compact first-contact argument force a common tangent line across the two adjacent branches. This contradicts adjacent interior slope-range separation (or endpoint tangent exclusion in the endpoint case). Therefore no such adjacent pair-pair line exists.

That is the payoff. It gives you adjacent pair-pair exclusion across generic turning boundaries without full symmetric zero-capture.
4. Why this is better than full zero-capture

Because it only uses the zeros that actually arise from a genuine support-overlap / pair-pair family. It does not require any theorem about all other possible kernel zeros on the whole open rectangle I2√óI1I2‚Äč√óI1‚Äč or I1√óI2I1‚Äč√óI2‚Äč.

So the remaining burden becomes:

    prove the strip exclusion lemma carefully,

    package the compact first-contact theorem for both directions,

    then replace the current ‚Äúconditional symmetric directional-kernel exclusion‚ÄĚ with this more targeted unconditional adjacent pair-pair exclusion theorem.

That is much more plausible than full symmetric zero-capture.
5. What I would do next

I would now try to freeze exactly these three statements in paper notation:

    Strip exclusion for K21K21‚Äč against a compact opposite branch
    and its mirror for K12K12‚Äč;

    Compact first-contact implies tangency
    for zeros of K21K21‚Äč or K12K12‚Äč on a compact box with a zero-free boundary-facing collar;

    Adjacent pair-pair exclusion across a generic turning boundary
    by combining the bridge lemma with the two items above.

This is the first route that looks like a genuine closure of the turning-boundary geometry without needing the full old zero-capture theorem.

\begin{lemma}[Strip exclusion for \(K_{21}\) against a compact opposite branch]
\label{lem:strip-exclusion-k21}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\) be a compact subinterval. Then there exists a one-sided
neighborhood \(U_2\subset I_2\) of the shared boundary point \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t):=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t))\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in U_2,\ t\in J_1),
\]
where \(F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h))\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Work in the affine chart
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
y(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}.
\]
At a generic turning boundary one has
\[
x'(h_b)=0,\qquad x''(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad y'(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Hence \(y\) is a valid local coordinate and the normalized curve may be written
near \(h_b\) as a graph
\[
x=\chi(y)
\]
with
\[
\chi'(y_b)=0,\qquad \chi''(y_b)\neq 0.
\]
Thus \(x\) has a strict local extremum at the boundary, while the tangent slope
\[
m(h)=\frac{dy/dh}{dx/dh}=\frac{N(h)}{M(h)}
\]
blows up one-sidedly as \(h\to h_b\) from either adjacent interval.

Because \(J_1\Subset I_1\) is compact away from \(h_b\), there exists
\(\delta>0\) such that
\[
|x(t)-x(h_b)|\ge 2\delta
\qquad
(t\in J_1).
\]
After shrinking to a small one-sided neighborhood \(U_2\subset I_2\) of \(h_b\),
we may also assume
\[
|x(s)-x(h_b)|\le \delta
\qquad
(s\in U_2).
\]
Hence
\[
|x(t)-x(s)|\ge \delta
\qquad
(s\in U_2,\ t\in J_1).
\]

Fix a preliminary compact neighborhood \(U_2^0\Subset I_2\) of \(h_b\) and set
\[
B:=\sup\{|y(t)-y(s)|:\ t\in J_1,\ s\in U_2^0\}<\infty.
\]
Since \(m(s)\to \pm\infty\) as \(s\to h_b\) in \(I_2\), we may shrink further to
\(U_2\subset U_2^0\) so that
\[
|m(s)|>\frac{2B}{\delta}
\qquad
(s\in U_2).
\]

If \(K_{21}(s,t)=0\), then the tangent line at \(\Gamma(s)\) passes through
\(\Gamma(t)\), equivalently
\[
y(t)-y(s)=m(s)\bigl(x(t)-x(s)\bigr).
\]
Taking absolute values gives
\[
B\ge |y(t)-y(s)|=|m(s)|\,|x(t)-x(s)|>\frac{2B}{\delta}\cdot \delta=2B,
\]
a contradiction. Therefore \(K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0\) on \(U_2\times J_1\).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Strip exclusion for \(K_{12}\) against a compact opposite branch]
\label{lem:strip-exclusion-k12}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Let \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be a compact subinterval. Then there exists a one-sided
neighborhood \(U_1\subset I_1\) of the shared boundary point \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s):=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s))\neq 0
\qquad
(t\in U_1,\ s\in J_2),
\]
where \(F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h))\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
This is the left-right mirror of
Lemma~\ref{lem:strip-exclusion-k21}, exchanging the roles of \(I_1\) and \(I_2\).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Compact first-contact for \(K_{21}\)]
\label{lem:compact-first-contact-k21}
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\), \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be compact subintervals across a
generic turning boundary. Assume there is a relatively open collar
\(V_2\subset J_2\) at the boundary-facing end of \(J_2\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in V_2,\ t\in J_1).
\]
Assume also that the zero set of \(K_{21}\) in \(J_2\times J_1\) is nonempty.
Then there exists a zero \((s_*,t_*)\in J_2\times J_1\) such that:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(s_*\) is boundary-nearest in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all
\(s\in J_2\) for which \(K_{21}(s,t)=0\) for some \(t\in J_1\);
\item
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Define
\[
A_{21}:=\{\,s\in J_2:\ \exists\, t\in J_1 \text{ with } K_{21}(s,t)=0\,\}.
\]
Since the zero set of \(K_{21}\) in \(J_2\times J_1\) is compact and nonempty,
the set \(A_{21}\) is compact and nonempty. By hypothesis,
\[
A_{21}\cap V_2=\varnothing.
\]
Hence \(A_{21}\) has a boundary-nearest element \(s_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\)
ordering on \(J_2\). Choose \(t_*\in J_1\) with
\[
K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]

Suppose
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)\neq 0.
\]
Then by the implicit function theorem there is a \(C^1\) local zero curve
\[
t=t(s)
\]
through \((s_*,t_*)\), defined for \(s\) near \(s_*\), such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t(s))=0.
\]
Because \(s_*\notin V_2\) and \(V_2\) is an open collar at the boundary-facing
end of \(J_2\), there exist nearby points \(s\in J_2\) that are boundary-nearer
than \(s_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering. For such \(s\), the point
\((s,t(s))\) is also a zero of \(K_{21}\), contradicting the minimality of
\(s_*\). Therefore
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Compact first-contact for \(K_{12}\)]
\label{lem:compact-first-contact-k12}
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\), \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be compact subintervals across a
generic turning boundary. Assume there is a relatively open collar
\(V_1\subset J_1\) at the boundary-facing end of \(J_1\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\qquad
(t\in V_1,\ s\in J_2).
\]
Assume also that the zero set of \(K_{12}\) in \(J_1\times J_2\) is nonempty.
Then there exists a zero \((t_*,s_*)\in J_1\times J_2\) such that:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(t_*\) is boundary-nearest in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all
\(t\in J_1\) for which \(K_{12}(t,s)=0\) for some \(s\in J_2\);
\item
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Define
\[
A_{12}:=\{\,t\in J_1:\ \exists\, s\in J_2 \text{ with } K_{12}(t,s)=0\,\}.
\]
Since the zero set of \(K_{12}\) in \(J_1\times J_2\) is compact and nonempty,
the set \(A_{12}\) is compact and nonempty. By hypothesis,
\[
A_{12}\cap V_1=\varnothing.
\]
Hence \(A_{12}\) has a boundary-nearest element \(t_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\)
ordering on \(J_1\). Choose \(s_*\in J_2\) with
\[
K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]

Suppose
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)\neq 0.
\]
Then by the implicit function theorem there is a \(C^1\) local zero curve
\[
s=s(t)
\]
through \((t_*,s_*)\), defined for \(t\) near \(t_*\), such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s(t))=0.
\]
Because \(t_*\notin V_1\) and \(V_1\) is an open collar at the boundary-facing
end of \(J_1\), there exist nearby points \(t\in J_1\) that are boundary-nearer
than \(t_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering. For such \(t\), the point
\((t,s(t))\) is also a zero of \(K_{12}\), contradicting the minimality of
\(t_*\). Therefore
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Adjacent pair-pair exclusion across a generic turning boundary]
\label{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Then no finite-slope affine line in the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c}
\]
is \(2\)-secant to both \(\Gamma(I_1)\) and \(\Gamma(I_2)\).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Assume for contradiction that a finite-slope line
\[
L:\ y=mx+b
\]
is \(2\)-secant to both adjacent branches. By
Lemma~\ref{lem:pair-pair-gives-directional-kernel-zero}, after removing a
small boundary neighborhood there exist compact subintervals
\[
J_1\Subset I_1,\qquad J_2\Subset I_2
\]
and a zero of one of the two directional kernels.

First suppose there exist \(s_0\in J_2\), \(t_0\in J_1\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s_0,t_0)=0.
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:strip-exclusion-k21}, there exists a one-sided neighborhood
\(U_2\subset I_2\) of \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in U_2,\ t\in J_1).
\]
Choose a compact interval \(\widetilde J_2\Subset I_2\) containing \(s_0\) and
whose boundary-facing collar \(V_2\) lies in \(U_2\). Then the zero set of
\(K_{21}\) in \(\widetilde J_2\times J_1\) is nonempty, and
Lemma~\ref{lem:compact-first-contact-k21} yields a zero
\[
(s_*,t_*)\in \widetilde J_2\times J_1
\]
such that
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]
Together with \(K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0\), this means that the tangent line at
\(\Gamma(s_*)\) is also tangent at \(\Gamma(t_*)\). Since
\(s_*\in I_2\) and \(t_*\in I_1\) are interior points, this gives a common
interior tangent slope across the adjacent branches, contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}.

Now suppose instead there exist \(t_0\in J_1\), \(s_0\in J_2\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t_0,s_0)=0.
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:strip-exclusion-k12}, there exists a one-sided neighborhood
\(U_1\subset I_1\) of \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\qquad
(t\in U_1,\ s\in J_2).
\]
Choose a compact interval \(\widetilde J_1\Subset I_1\) containing \(t_0\) and
whose boundary-facing collar \(V_1\) lies in \(U_1\). Then the zero set of
\(K_{12}\) in \(\widetilde J_1\times J_2\) is nonempty, and
Lemma~\ref{lem:compact-first-contact-k12} yields a zero
\[
(t_*,s_*)\in \widetilde J_1\times J_2
\]
such that
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
Together with \(K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0\), this means that the tangent line at
\(\Gamma(t_*)\) is also tangent at \(\Gamma(s_*)\). Again this gives a common
interior tangent slope across the adjacent branches, contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}.

Both cases are impossible. Therefore no finite-slope line is \(2\)-secant to
both adjacent branches across a generic turning boundary.
\end{proof}

I can draft the theorem you would need, but I do not think we can honestly mark it proved from the current ingredients yet.

The exact propagation theorem that would let you replace thm:partial-global-no-secant by the new route is this:

\begin{theorem}[Propagation to an adjacent turning pair]
\label{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair}
Let
\[
I_1<I_2<\cdots<I_N
\]
be the ECT intervals on a connected generic component of the normalized curve,
and assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item every internal boundary between adjacent intervals is either
a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0,
\]
or a finite-order boundary of type
\[
W(h_b)=0,\qquad M(h_b)\neq 0;
\]

\item on every connected open subinterval with \(c\neq 0\) and \(M\neq 0\),
one has \(W\not\equiv 0\);

\item every finite-order boundary of type \(W=0,\ M\neq 0\) is locally sterile
for pair-pair birth in the sense of
Theorem~\ref{thm:finite-order-w-zero-local-sterility};
\end{enumerate}

Fix a finite-slope affine line
\[
L_{m,b}:\ y=mx+b.
\]
If \(L_{m,b}\) is \(2\)-secant to \(\Gamma(I_a)\) and to \(\Gamma(I_b)\) for
some \(a<b\), then there exists an index \(j\) with
\[
a\le j<b
\]
such that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(I_j<I_{j+1}\) meet at a generic turning boundary; and
\item \(L_{m,b}\) is \(2\)-secant to both \(\Gamma(I_j)\) and \(\Gamma(I_{j+1})\).
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}[Global no-four-secant from adjacent turning exclusion]
\label{cor:global-no-four-secant-from-adjacent-turning}
Assume the hypotheses of
Theorem~\ref{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair}, and assume in addition
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning}. Then no finite-slope
affine line in the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c}
\]
is \(2\)-secant to two distinct ECT intervals.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
If such a line existed, Theorem~\ref{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair}
would produce an adjacent pair across a generic turning boundary to which the
line is \(2\)-secant on both sides, contradicting
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning}.
\end{proof}

That is the right theorem if your goal is to replace the old thm:partial-global-no-secant route with the new turning-boundary route.

But I want to be clear: the hard content is not the corollary. It is the propagation theorem itself. And inside that theorem, there is one genuinely nontrivial sublemma.

The sublemma you need is this:

\begin{lemma}[Turning birth forces adjacent persistence]
\label{lem:turning-birth-forces-adjacent-persistence}
Under the hypotheses of
Theorem~\ref{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair},
let \(L_{m,b}\) be a finite-slope line and let
\[
\Sigma_{m,b}:=\{\,j:\ L_{m,b}\text{ is \(2\)-secant to }\Gamma(I_j)\,\}.
\]
Suppose \(j\) is the leftmost index in \(\Sigma_{m,b}\), and suppose the
boundary between \(I_j\) and \(I_{j+1}\) is generic turning. Then
\[
j+1\in \Sigma_{m,b}.
\]
Equivalently, once \(2\)-secancy is present at the first interval of a minimal
bad block, it persists across the first internal generic turning boundary.
\end{lemma}

That lemma is the real hinge. If you had it, the propagation theorem would be very believable:

    choose a pair-pair line hitting two intervals with minimal span;

    let aa be the leftmost and bb the rightmost 22-secant intervals;

    finite-order W=0W=0 local sterility and no WW-flat intervals tell you that the change from ‚Äúnot 22-secant‚ÄĚ to ‚Äú22-secant‚ÄĚ cannot be born at a W=0, M‚Č†0W=0, MÓÄ†=0 boundary;

    so the first birth must occur at a generic turning boundary;

    the lemma above then forces persistence into the next interval, giving an adjacent turning pair with 2+22+2 secancy.

That is exactly what would let the new adjacent theorem replace the old partial global theorem.

So my honest recommendation is:

    do not insert the propagation theorem as proved yet;

    insert it only as a target theorem / roadmap theorem if you want;

    the actual next math attack should be on
    Lemma \reflem:turning‚ąíbirth‚ąíforces‚ąíadjacent‚ąípersistence\reflem:turning‚ąíbirth‚ąíforces‚ąíadjacent‚ąípersistence.

That is the precise new frontier. If you want, I can now try to attack that lemma directly and see whether it really goes through or whether another obstruction appears.

stop, I don't want latex. do the math on this lemma

I think the first thing to check is whether the lemma is even true as stated. There is an immediate tension: if a line is already 2-secant to IjIj‚Äč and the next boundary is generic turning, ‚Äúj+1‚ąąő£j+1‚ąąő£‚ÄĚ would force an adjacent 2+22+2 pair, which your new turning-boundary theorem forbids. So I‚Äôm testing whether the lemma needs extra hypotheses or should be reformulated.

I do not think that lemma is true as stated.

The proposed lemma was:

‚ÄúLet Sigma_(m,b) be the set of indices j such that the line y = m x + b is 2-secant to branch I_j. If j is the leftmost index in Sigma and the boundary between I_j and I_(j+1) is a generic turning boundary, then j+1 is also in Sigma.‚ÄĚ

That is too strong.

Why it fails:

What you currently know at a generic turning boundary is only a local exclusion of adjacent pair-pair. You do not know a global persistence law for fixed-slope intercept intervals. In support-interval language, for a fixed slope m, each branch has an interval B_j(m) of allowed intercepts. The new turning-boundary theorem says adjacent branches cannot both have b in the interior of their support intervals. But that does not force overlap to persist from B_j(m) into B_(j+1)(m).

Combinatorially, there is no contradiction in having, for one fixed slope m,

B_j(m) = (0,2),
B_(j+1)(m) = (3,4),
B_(j+2)(m) = (1,2).

Then b = 1.5 gives 2-secancy on j and j+2, but not on j+1. That already shows the persistence statement is not a consequence of ‚Äúadjacent interiors are disjoint.‚ÄĚ

And this is not just an abstract interval trick. Geometrically, you can realize that behavior with three strictly convex branches in x-order, joined by generic turning boundaries, where a line of fixed slope cuts the first and third branches twice but misses the middle branch as a 2-secant. Nothing in the current local turning-boundary theorem forbids that.

There is also a structural warning sign: if the lemma were true, then together with your new theorem excluding adjacent pair-pair across a generic turning boundary, you would immediately get something much stronger than you currently have ‚ÄĒ essentially a direct global no-four-secant result on any chain of turning boundaries ‚ÄĒ without needing the old support-ordering machinery. That is exactly the kind of ‚Äútoo good to be true‚ÄĚ jump that usually signals the propagation lemma is overstated.

So I would not try to prove that lemma. I think the right conclusion is:

The ‚Äúfirst birth persists into the next branch‚ÄĚ statement is false, or at least far stronger than what the current ingredients justify.

What is true, and much closer to your actual machinery, is weaker:

If a line is 2-secant to two non-adjacent branches, then as you move from left to right across the chain of intervals, there is a first boundary where the fixed intercept b enters the support set. At that boundary, the entry cannot happen through a finite-order W=0, M ‚Č† 0 boundary by the local sterility theorem. So the first entry must occur at a generic turning boundary, and it occurs by tangential birth on one side, not necessarily by immediate 2-secancy on the next branch.

That weaker statement is exactly compatible with your current bridge lemmas:

    not ‚Äúj+1 is also 2-secant,‚ÄĚ

    but ‚Äúat the first relevant turning boundary, one gets support contact / a directional kernel zero.‚ÄĚ

That is a very different propagation mechanism.

So the corrected next target should be something like this:

For a fixed line y = m x + b, suppose it is 2-secant to two distinct branches, and choose such a pair with minimal index gap. Then there exists an internal generic turning boundary between them such that b lies in the support set of both adjacent branches at that boundary, with one side realizing b as an endpoint of its support interval. Equivalently, the overlap is born there by tangency, giving a directional kernel zero.

That statement is believable from the present ingredients. It matches:

    your finite-order W=0 sterility theorem,

    your compact support-overlap truncation lemma,

    your overlap-endpoint-to-directional-kernel-zero lemma,

    and the new unconditional adjacent pair-pair exclusion across a generic turning boundary.

But it does not give immediate adjacent 2-secancy.

So my math verdict is:

    the lemma you proposed is the wrong propagation lemma;

    the true propagation mechanism is ‚Äúfirst entry into support at a turning boundary gives tangency / kernel zero,‚ÄĚ not ‚Äúfirst entry gives immediate 2-secancy on the next branch.‚ÄĚ

That is the direction I would push now. It is also much more consistent with the current draft‚Äôs actual proven tools.

ok, hang on, suppose we prove ‚Äúfirst entry into support at a turning boundary gives tangency / kernel zero,‚ÄĚ --> what does this give us

If you can prove
‚Äúfirst entry into support at a turning boundary ‚üĻ tangency / directional-kernel zero,‚ÄĚ
‚Äúfirst entry into support at a turning boundary ‚üĻ tangency / directional-kernel zero,‚ÄĚ

then what it gives you is not immediate global no-four-secant, but it gives you the missing bridge from a hypothetical non-adjacent pair-pair line to an adjacent turning-boundary obstruction.

That is exactly the kind of bridge the current draft still lacks. The draft‚Äôs own bottleneck language is still essentially ‚Äúadjacent kernel sign regularity would imply global no-four-secant,‚ÄĚ so a theorem that forces any first support entry to manifest as an adjacent turning-boundary tangency would be a real replacement for that missing adjacent step.

Concretely, it would give this chain:

    Assume a finite-slope line Lm,bLm,b‚Äč is 22-secant to two distinct ECT branches.

    Choose such a pair with minimal index gap.

    Move from left to right across the chain of branches and look at the fixed-slope support intervals
    BIj(m)=Ōēm(Ij),Ōēm(h)=őĒ7(h)‚ąímv5(h)c(h).
    BIj‚Äč‚Äč(m)=Ōēm‚Äč(Ij‚Äč),Ōēm‚Äč(h)=c(h)őĒ7‚Äč(h)‚ąímv5‚Äč(h)‚Äč.

    Since the line is not 22-secant to the earlier branches, there is a first boundary where the intercept bb enters the support picture.

    Your finite-order W=0, M‚Č†0W=0, MÓÄ†=0 theorem plus the no-WW-flatness result say this first entry cannot be explained by generic W=0W=0 local birth.

    Therefore the first entry must occur at a generic turning boundary.

    If your new theorem says that this first entry forces tangency / a zero of one directional kernel, then the current turning-boundary machinery becomes relevant right there.

    If, in addition, you have the new unconditional adjacent pair-pair exclusion across generic turning boundaries, then that first-entry event is impossible.

    Hence no such non-adjacent pair-pair line can exist.

So the payoff is:

it would let you propagate a hypothetical global pair-pair obstruction down to an adjacent turning-boundary contradiction.

That is the right shape of a replacement for the old thm:partial-global-no-secant route.

But there is one important subtlety:

It only works if the ‚Äúfirst entry‚ÄĚ theorem is strong enough to produce adjacent turning-boundary tangency in a way that interacts with the new unconditional adjacent theorem, not just some vague support contact. In practice, you want the output to be one of these:

    a genuine directional-kernel zero on an adjacent pair, or

    an actual adjacent pair-pair line across that turning boundary.

If you get only ‚Äúsupport endpoint contact‚ÄĚ with no way to upgrade it, that is not enough.

So the right corrected propagation theorem is not:

    ‚Äú2-secancy persists into the next branch.‚ÄĚ

That one is too strong and likely false.

It is instead:

    ‚ÄúFor a minimal-gap pair-pair line, the first support entry occurs at an adjacent generic turning boundary and is realized by tangency / directional-kernel contact there.‚ÄĚ

If you can prove that, then together with your current unconditional adjacent turning theorem, you really do get a plausible new global route.

So the short answer is:

What it gives you is a reduction of any global pair-pair obstruction to an adjacent turning-boundary event.

ok let's push the new propagation theorem now!

And the same issue survives if you strengthen ‚Äúsupport‚ÄĚ to ‚Äú2-secancy‚ÄĚ: first entry into Sj(m)Sj‚Äč(m) can still happen on branch jj without being born at the adjacent boundary. It can just be a global feature of that branch.

    You still do not have a theorem that reduces nonadjacent pair-pair to the adjacent case.

What it gives immediately:

    no three nearby points across a generic turning boundary are collinear;

    no 3-secant or 4-secant line can be created locally there;

    the determinant kernel has the same kind of rigid sign behavior across a turning boundary that it has on a single WW-sign interval.

Now the important caveat:

But that is okay, because the finite-order W=0W=0 theorem gives something different: local sterility. So the picture is now:

    on each interval where WW keeps sign: exact determinant sign law by the mean-value theorem;

    across a generic turning boundary: local determinant sign law by the argument above;

    across a finite-order W=0, M‚Č†0W=0, MÓÄ†=0 boundary: no sign law, but no local pair-pair birth.

That is the real output of the attack.

So the obstacle has shifted again.

The remaining hard part is no longer ‚Äúcan we transport the sign across a turning boundary?‚ÄĚ
We can.

The remaining hard part is:

That is a more precise problem. It is not a purely local sign-transport problem anymore. It is a global chain/continuation problem combining:

    turning-boundary sign-regular bridges,

    and W=0W=0 sterile transitions.

That is a much tighter frontier than before.

Now here is the theorem you can prove from the current ingredients.
